<span id="freeTextContainer7627317404919195302">When James accidentally drops some magic crystals by the old peach tree, strange things start to happen. The peach at the top of the tree begins to grow, and before long it's as big as a house. When James discovers a secret entranceway into the fruit and crawls inside, he meets wonderful new friends--the Old-Green-Grasshopper, the dainty Ladybug, and the Centipede of the m</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7627317404919195302">When James accidentally drops some magic crystals by the old peach tree, strange things start to happen. The peach at the top of the tree begins to grow, and before long it's as big as a house. When James discovers a secret entranceway into the fruit and crawls inside, he meets wonderful new friends--the Old-Green-Grasshopper, the dainty Ladybug, and the Centipede of the multiple boots. After years of feeling like an outsider in his aunts' house, James finally found a place where he belongs. With a snip of the stem, the peach household starts rolling away--and the adventure begins!<br /><p>Roald Dahl's first and most widely celebrated book for young people continues to thrill readers around the world.<br /></p><p>"This is a stunning book to be cherished for its story, a superb fantasy."--<em>Chicago Tribune</em><br /></p><p>"A beautifully written, fantastic book."--<em>Christian Science Monitor</em></p></span>

  <a href="/user/show/18974711-darth-j" class="left imgcol" title="Darth J"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1442508603p2/18974711.jpg" alt="Darth J" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1369845164?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 21, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/18974711-darth-j" class="user" title="Darth J">Darth J</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1369845164">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2417296832113404255">For a brief period in my childhood, I was obsessed with this. After seeing the trailer, I noticed that the movie trailer tie-in edition was available in the next month's book order form so I had to have it to read before I saw it.<br /><img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1424457097i/13764128._SX540_.jpg" /><br /><br />Then I saw the movie and of course, really loved it. It was whimsical and visually appealing, even if it did deviate from the source material in parts.<br /><img class="escapedImg" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1440161548ra/15939171.gif" /><br /><br />I even remember wanting to only eat and drink peach stuff for a while, and since it was the 90's I had to have my Snapp</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2417296832113404255">For a brief period in my childhood, I was obsessed with this. After seeing the trailer, I noticed that the movie trailer tie-in edition was available in the next month's book order form so I had to have it to read before I saw it.<br /><img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1424457097i/13764128._SX540_.jpg" /><br /><br />Then I saw the movie and of course, really loved it. It was whimsical and visually appealing, even if it did deviate from the source material in parts.<br /><img class="escapedImg" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1440161548ra/15939171.gif" /><br /><br />I even remember wanting to only eat and drink peach stuff for a while, and since it was the 90's I had to have my Snapple Peach Iced Tea.<br /><img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1440161548i/15939172.jpg" /><br /><br />So that's it, just some warm nostalgia I will always have for this book.<br /></span>

        rated it

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer29269589">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7407306574718387625">When I was in third grade, the first great crush of my life, Scott Murphy, stood up on the picnic table outside of the trailer that housed my third grade class and instead of reading his lines -- James' words -- from his paperback copy while we were all trying to rehearse the chapter we were supposed to present to the entire class the next day, he performed the most passionate version of "Paradise City" by Guns N Roses that I have ever seen.<br /><br />To this day, I love Gun N Roses, I love Scott Murphy, a</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7407306574718387625">When I was in third grade, the first great crush of my life, Scott Murphy, stood up on the picnic table outside of the trailer that housed my third grade class and instead of reading his lines -- James' words -- from his paperback copy while we were all trying to rehearse the chapter we were supposed to present to the entire class the next day, he performed the most passionate version of "Paradise City" by Guns N Roses that I have ever seen.<br /><br />To this day, I love Gun N Roses, I love Scott Murphy, and I love this book. Magical.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_641244361">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/641244361" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/13400128-lynda" class="left imgcol" title="Lynda"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1349428443p2/13400128.jpg" alt="Lynda" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/641244361?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 23, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/13400128-lynda" class="user" title="Lynda">Lynda</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/17242185-james-and-the-giant-peach" title="Paperback 9780141346311" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Children and adults alike</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Lynda by: </span>
      Banned Books Group

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a href="/review/list/13400128-lynda?shelf=childrens-literature" class="actionLinkLite">childrens-literature</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer641244361">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12416332241827370013"><strong>James and the Giant Peach - B A N N E D!</strong><br /><br /><img width="400" height="300" class="escapedImg" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/photos/1416737051p8/1094218.jpg" /><br />Recently I joined the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/group/show/217-banned-books">Banned Books group</a> and one of the group reads for this month was <a title="James and the Giant Peach by Roald Dahl" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6689.James_and_the_Giant_Peach">James and the Giant Peach</a>. I'm sure there are many GR readers who have read a Roald Dahl book and/or seen a movie adaptation of one of his books. If you have, then you would know that Dahl has consistently written stories that entertain children with morals and life lessons that even adults can appreciate. <br /><br /><strong>So why was this book banned? </strong><br /><br />Let's first take a look at what Jame</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12416332241827370013"><strong>James and the Giant Peach - B A N N E D!</strong><br /><br /><img width="400" height="300" class="escapedImg" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/photos/1416737051p8/1094218.jpg" /><br />Recently I joined the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/group/show/217-banned-books">Banned Books group</a> and one of the group reads for this month was <a title="James and the Giant Peach by Roald Dahl" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6689.James_and_the_Giant_Peach">James and the Giant Peach</a>. I'm sure there are many GR readers who have read a Roald Dahl book and/or seen a movie adaptation of one of his books. If you have, then you would know that Dahl has consistently written stories that entertain children with morals and life lessons that even adults can appreciate. <br /><br /><strong>So why was this book banned? </strong><br /><br />Let's first take a look at what James and the Giant Peach is about...<br /><br /><u>The story</u><br /><br />This is a story about a boy named James Henry Trotter who is forced to live with his two abusive aunts (Aunt Sponge and Aunt Spiker) in the English countryside after his parents are tragically killed from a rhinoceros attack in London. <blockquote>
  <img width="200" height="150" class="escapedImg" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/photos/1416739275p8/1094233.jpg" />
</blockquote>James is a very sad and miserable little boy. One day, a mysterious old man presents James with a bag of tiny green crystals that have magical powers. James is told that once he swallows the crystals with water, marvellous, fabulous and unbelievable things will happen to him, and he'll never be miserable again. James rushes back to the house to get some water and accidentally falls and spills the tiny green crystals near a dormant peach tree which sits on a hill nearby. The lifeless tree suddenly sprouts a peach, which begins to grow larger and larger.<br /><br />His abusive aunts take notice of the giant peach. They fence it off and begin charging people money to see it. And after locking up James all day, the aunts force him to clean up after the crowds are gone. <blockquote>
  <img width="200" height="150" class="escapedImg" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/photos/1416739423p8/1094235.jpg" />
</blockquote>It is while James is cleaning up around the giant peach that he discovers a tunnel leading to the middle of it. There, he finds a group of human-sized insects: Centipede, Grasshopper, Spider, Earthworm, Ladybird, Silkworm, and Glow-worm. The group befriends James and attribute their giant growth to having eaten the tiny green crystals that James had spilled. <blockquote>
  <img width="150" height="100" class="escapedImg" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/photos/1416741139p8/1094248.jpg" /><img width="200" height="150" class="escapedImg" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/photos/1416740881p8/1094247.jpg" /><img width="150" height="100" class="escapedImg" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/photos/1416741563p8/1094252.jpg" /><img width="150" height="100" class="escapedImg" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/photos/1416745421p8/1094264.jpg" /><img width="150" height="100" class="escapedImg" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/photos/1416741291p8/1094250.jpg" /><img width="150" height="100" class="escapedImg" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/photos/1416741790p8/1094253.jpg" /><img width="150" height="100" class="escapedImg" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/photos/1416740398p8/1094242.jpg" />
</blockquote>Early the next day, the group decides to escape the horrible aunts by cutting the stem connecting the peach to the tree. The giant peach begins to roll down the hillside, flattening and killing James' two aunts in the process.<br /><br />The giant peach rolls faster and faster through towns, across land until finally it crashes into the ocean. To avoid a group of vicious sharks, James attaches strings (produced by the spider and silkworm) to hundreds of seagulls and the peach is lifted and carried in the air.<br /><br />As they are floating, they come across some Cloud-Men, mystic creatures who are responsible for the different types of weather, like rain, snow, and hail. And as they are drifting through the clouds, the peach crashes into a rainbow, which snaps some of the strings, and the peach begins to descend. As the peach approaches land, it crashes on top of the Empire State Building. <br /><br />In the end, <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[all of the insects become successful in America, and James, who has made a lot of friends, lives in the large peach pip in Central Park. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br /><u>Key themes</u><br /><br />This story urges readers to take a closer look at the world around them. First, a closer look at nature. There are several amazing things happening in the natural world, from photosynthesis to the water cycle. The book even explores the importance of each type of insect and how each of them contributes to the well-being of the environment.<br /><br />But this idea of looking closer at the world also applies to looking at other people and how we should take the time to talk to them to get to know them. In doing so, we can find out how they contribute to the rest of us since everyone contributes to the world.<br /><br />On a more hopeful note, the underlying message of this story is that there are ways to escape a troubling homelife. The verbal and emotional abuse James receives is a bit over-the-top, but that might be the reality for some kids. How do they escape that? And while they can't fly away on a giant peach, ultimately, this story suggests that abused kids can find possible escapes through friends and writing. That the power of relationships and creativity and imagination are all a person needs to take them anywhere they want to go i.e. it shows kids that no matter how bad things may seem, or how bad they get, there is always hope.<br /><br /><u>So why was James and the Giant Peach banned?</u><br /><br />It was banned as being too scary for the targeted age group, mysticism, sexual inferences, profanity, racism, references to tobacco and alcohol, and claims that it promotes disobedience, drugs, and communism.<br /><br />A challenge was brought before the school council in Indian River County, Florida, because of the story’s mystical elements involving magic crocodile tongues which enchanted the peach tree.<br /><br />The Times of London reported that it was once banned in a Wisconsin town because a reference to a spider licking her lips could be <em>“taken in two ways, including sexual.”</em><br /><br /><img width="350" height="400" class="escapedImg" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/photos/1416744648p8/1094262.jpg" /><br /><em>That Times statement conjured up this image!! haha :)) </em><br /><br />Other challenges involve repeated use of the word “ass,” which resulted in a 1991 challenge in Altoona, Wisconsin. The following year, a woman in Hernando County, Florida, took issue with Grasshopper’s statement <blockquote>
  <em>“I’d rather be fried alive and eaten by a Mexican!”</em>
</blockquote> as well as references to snuff, tobacco and whiskey. Her complaints to her 10-year-old daughter’s school principal led to a review by the regional school board.<br /><br />In a blog by Madeline Holler, she took issue not with the language or drugs, but jokes about physical characteristics because of the physical description of James’ cruel guardians Aunt Sponge and Aunt Spiker. One is very fat and one is very skinny and their features are laughed at, criticized, and meant to be evoke disgust.<br /><br /><strong>Conclusion</strong><br /><br />Book challenges and banning is proof to the power of good literature, creative language, and original imagery. While some might have issues with certain realities of the world that children are growing up in; and as troubled as it makes adults to be reminded of these facts as they attempt to shield their children from harm, children’s literature is a great way for them to get a glimpse at the issues that they WILL have to deal with some day.<br /><br />I can’t think of a single book from my childhood that distorted my morality or sense of self. I was also lucky enough to have parents who didn’t shield me from the darker aspects of life. If I had an issue or question, it was talked about openly and honestly.<br /><br /><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="12416332241827370013">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_833034712">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/833034712" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/922495-patrick" class="left imgcol" title="Patrick"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1351307095p2/922495.jpg" alt="Patrick" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/833034712?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 24, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/922495-patrick" class="user" title="Patrick">Patrick</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer833034712">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1687122727147250652">I'm going to be completely honest here. I didn't like this book. <br /><br />Usually, if I don't care for a book, I keep schtum about it here. Not only is it not as much fun to talk about books I didn't like, but bad-mouthing other author's books is pretty rude. <br /><br />But, given that Dahl has passed on, and a fair number of people ask me for book recommendations for their kids. I figured I'd chime in with my opinion about this one. <br /><br />I recently read it with my son, and while it wasn't *awful,* it was just... Meh. I</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1687122727147250652">I'm going to be completely honest here. I didn't like this book. <br /><br />Usually, if I don't care for a book, I keep schtum about it here. Not only is it not as much fun to talk about books I didn't like, but bad-mouthing other author's books is pretty rude. <br /><br />But, given that Dahl has passed on, and a fair number of people ask me for book recommendations for their kids. I figured I'd chime in with my opinion about this one. <br /><br />I recently read it with my son, and while it wasn't *awful,* it was just... Meh. It was just a series of vaguely interesting events loosely connected by not much of anything. <br /><br />And no. It's not a Picaresque. You know what a picaresque is. And if you do, it doesn't matter because that's not an excuse for a meh book with no narrative through line. So don't bring that weak shit around here. <br /><br />Here's the thing, before this book, I read Charlie and the Chocolate Factory to my boy. *That* was an interesting book. Charlie was in a bad place. He was a sympathetic character. He *wanted* something for himself. He strove for it. And when he got it, we, the readers, were delighted. <br /><br />James... not so much. <br /><br />In Charlie and the Chocolate factory, each of the rooms was kind of cool and interesting. People talked *about* things. <br /><br />IN this book, about 60% most of the dialogue was just four different people exclaiming about the same thing. Or they'd bicker pointlessly about the same things. There were some notable exceptions, but for the most part it was purposeless, and tedious to read. <br /><br />Honestly, I skipped pieces of it. And my son, who has read the book before with his mother, didn't notice. (Because he *will* call me out on stuff like that.) <br /><br />Now could a child enjoy reading this book on their own? Absolutely. Some of the language will be a a little opaque for a young reader, but I think that's good. That stretches your mind and builds vocabulary. <br /><br />But as a book you're thinking of reading to a kid? I'd pass. There are so many other better options out there. Books with more engaging characters and more compelling stories.... </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1687122727147250652">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_492455397">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/492455397" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/6610433-midnightfaerie" class="left imgcol" title="midnightfaerie"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1318372863p2/6610433.jpg" alt="midnightfaerie" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/492455397?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 22, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/6610433-midnightfaerie" class="user" title="midnightfaerie">midnightfaerie</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/6610433-midnightfaerie?shelf=childrens" class="actionLinkLite">childrens</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/6610433-midnightfaerie?shelf=classics" class="actionLinkLite">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer492455397">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17119527891598893812">I read James and the Giant Peach by Roald Dahl with my five year old. We had just finished Charlotte's Web and I was looking for chapter books I could enjoy as well. And I knew we had the movie so we could watch it when we were done. It turned out to be a great choice. Dahl uses the cliffhanger method at the end of almost every one of his chapters, and each chapter is only a page or two long. That combined with great illustrations, made for a win-win situation. I often ended a chapter only to ha</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17119527891598893812">I read James and the Giant Peach by Roald Dahl with my five year old. We had just finished Charlotte's Web and I was looking for chapter books I could enjoy as well. And I knew we had the movie so we could watch it when we were done. It turned out to be a great choice. Dahl uses the cliffhanger method at the end of almost every one of his chapters, and each chapter is only a page or two long. That combined with great illustrations, made for a win-win situation. I often ended a chapter only to have my son beg for another. This is a good sign, and something he didn't do with Charlotte's Web, even though he enjoyed it too. I also picked it because it was something I had never read. Turns out it was the perfect selection all around. Only took us a few weeks to read it and it was highly enjoyable. The characters come alive in a way other stories about bugs never did for me. I actually found myself sympathizing with the earthworm. The imagination and magic factor held me the whole time, and my son and I often discussed what would happen next. Some of our ideas were so far out there, but we laughed, knowing it was highly possible, even probable that they could very well be true. Dahl's penchant for silliness and the unexpected made our guesses that much more realistic. <br /><br />The vocabulary was perfect, just enough to keep me from being bored, but easy enough for my son to understand, and we both learned new words! His was peach stone, another name for a peach pit. Mine was steeplejacks, and no, I won't tell you what it is, you have to look it up. Dahl's writing is fantastic, and I had great fun making up tunes to his songs throughout the book. The book definitely has the longevity factor going for it, and children can appreciate the many themes of friendship and loyalty for the duration the book. So in these regards I think it can be considered a classic. <br /><br />The only complaint that some may have about this book is that he uses the word "ass" a few times in it, and even though I was told about it, I was still surprised when I stumbled over their use. Because I was reading this to my son, it wasn't a problem, I just substituted a word for it or skipped over it. If a child is reading the book on their own, well, I'm a big believer that if the adult cares so much, they should be reading the books first and then discussing it with their kids. Much of the great literature that's out there has ideas that are taboo in polite circles or are difficult concepts to explain when looking at human history. But that's what makes them great. And it's much better to educate your child, and do it yourself, before they get misconstrued opinions from the world at large or other teachers. I remember many teachers I had that pushed their opinions, good and bad on students when reading literature. That's a parent's responsibility. As for this book, it was excellent. And I highly recommend it for your first chapter books for your child. <br /><br />Now for the movie. I was excited because this was the first time my son had read a book and seen the movie afterward. It was funny to see how disappointed he was. That sounds bad. What I mean is, he enjoyed it and we had a fun "popcorn night" that is one of his favorite things, but his favorite part of the movie was the part about the sharks. He was like, "There wasn't a robot shark in the book! Where are the real sharks? And what happened to the cloud men?" I laughed. I told him it often happens that the movie is nothing like the book and then asked which he liked better. He vehemently exclaimed "The book!" Which, of course, was the whole objective. Lesson learned. <br /><br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" href="http://classicsdefined.com/giantpeach.html"> ClassicsDefined.com</a></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17119527891598893812">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer14229404560323843005">A funny, dark and poetic book. I read this after seeing a documentary about Roald Dahls' life and hearing some of the book's passages narrated within the perspective of his time cramped up in a WWII bomber plane as a bombardier (he was very tall). The peach represents the polar opposite of being in a noisy and clattering war plane, manning a gun and always under the threat of death. The peach is a peaceful, sweet and quiet flying machine.</span>

  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/129818090" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/796425-jeanette-astute-crabbist" class="left imgcol" title="Jeanette  &quot;Astute Crabbist&quot;"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1304653755p2/796425.jpg" alt="Jeanette  &quot;Astute Crabbist&quot;" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/129818090?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 24, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/796425-jeanette-astute-crabbist" class="user" title="Jeanette  &quot;Astute Crabbist&quot;">Jeanette  "Astute Crabbist"</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/232188.James_and_the_Giant_Peach" title="Paperback 9780140374247" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/796425-jeanette-astute-crabbist?shelf=books-little-me-loved" class="actionLinkLite">books-little-me-loved</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/796425-jeanette-astute-crabbist?shelf=five-star-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">five-star-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/796425-jeanette-astute-crabbist?shelf=all-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">all-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/796425-jeanette-astute-crabbist?shelf=for-the-munchkins" class="actionLinkLite">for-the-munchkins</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer129818090">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12197784283996193895">I've never visited Central Park, but if I ever do, I'll be watching for the giant peach pit where James Henry Trotter settled happily after his wild excursion. <br /><br />I knew I liked this book as a kid but I couldn't remember the details of the story. The thing that stayed in my mind all these years was the feeling of claustrophobia when James makes his way inside the peach and finds all the giant, friendly creepy-crawlies inside the peach pit. As a kid you always place yourself within the story, and I</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12197784283996193895">I've never visited Central Park, but if I ever do, I'll be watching for the giant peach pit where James Henry Trotter settled happily after his wild excursion. <br /><br />I knew I liked this book as a kid but I couldn't remember the details of the story. The thing that stayed in my mind all these years was the feeling of claustrophobia when James makes his way inside the peach and finds all the giant, friendly creepy-crawlies inside the peach pit. As a kid you always place yourself within the story, and I remember thinking how I'd hate to be in that enclosed space with all those critters and no windows. There was no fear of Old-Green-Grasshopper or Centipede or Earthworm or any of the others, just the feeling that if I were James I'd want OUT of that peach pit and into the fresh air. <br /><br />Reading it again as an adult, my strongest impressions are of just how much FUN this whole adventure is. Roald Dahl manages the perfect blend of whimsical, frightful, exciting, and tender elements. I think that's why his books can be every bit as appealing to adults as they are to children. I'd be laughing one minute at Centipede's songs about Aunts Sponge and Spiker, and grossing out a few minutes later when Centipede tells Earthworm that the Cloud-Men will eat him: "They would cut you up like a salami and eat you in thin slices." <br /><br />There's just so much to delight the reader in this first of Roald Dahl's books for children. There are the Cloud-Men who make the weather, the quirky and fractious creatures who share James's journey, the rainbow paint (LOVE the purple rainbow paint on the Centipede!), and of course the strange little man with the bristly black whiskers who gets the whole thing rolling with the "little green things." <br />"Where do they come from?" James wants to know. <br /><br />"Crocodile tongues! One thousand long slimy crocodile tongues boiled up in the skull of a dead witch for twenty days and nights with the eyeballs of a lizard! Add the fingers of a young monkey, the gizzard of a pig, the beak of a green parrot, the juice of a porcupine, and three spoonfuls of sugar. Stew for another week, and then let the moon do the rest!"<br /><br />Ah, Roald Dahl....You can still make my heart go pitter-pat with passages like that. <br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="12197784283996193895">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer4905013699367712862">I don't think I've heard of the Wangdoodle book. I'll have to take a look at it. So many books I missed out on as a kid! Oh well, it's never too late.</span>

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer409352891">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4705183300483656204">I remember when this book was read to me by my fifth grade teacher during our daily story time hour. I also remember hating that teacher because he played favorites to a group of girls that bullied me. However, despite my obvious distaste for the class and its inhabitants, I largely attribute that particular teacher for reading out to me some of my most favourite children's books; this particular Roald Dahl was one of them. <br /><br />In all honesty before re-reading this after some 14 years, the only part</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4705183300483656204">I remember when this book was read to me by my fifth grade teacher during our daily story time hour. I also remember hating that teacher because he played favorites to a group of girls that bullied me. However, despite my obvious distaste for the class and its inhabitants, I largely attribute that particular teacher for reading out to me some of my most favourite children's books; this particular Roald Dahl was one of them. <br /><br />In all honesty before re-reading this after some 14 years, the only part of the entire book that I remembered distinctly was the bit with the old man and the word "peculiar" repeated several times (it was the word we had to remember for a test in the class, I believe). So re-reading this was like a new experience, which was just completely exciting for me!<br /><br />This was such a short, sweet and lovely little book filled with all the classic kid lit tropes, which made it utterly enjoyable and as whimsical and delicious as the peach described in the book! I highly recommend it and I can gladly say that after fourteen years it still remains a favourite.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_69445850">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/69445850" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2602702-christina-a-reader-of-fictions" class="left imgcol" title="Christina (A Reader of Fictions)"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1430405357p2/2602702.jpg" alt="Christina (A Reader of Fictions)" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/69445850?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 01, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2602702-christina-a-reader-of-fictions" class="user" title="Christina (A Reader of Fictions)">Christina (A Reader of Fictions)</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/109137.James_and_the_Giant_Peach" title="Paperback 9780142410363" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/2602702-christina-a-reader-of-fictions?shelf=will-reread-forever" class="actionLinkLite">will-reread-forever</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2602702-christina-a-reader-of-fictions?shelf=on-my-shelves" class="actionLinkLite">on-my-shelves</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer69445850">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9237860542068256660">Oh, childhood, Roald Dahl takes me right back. I will always love Roald Dahl's work, because of how much these books meant to me as a kid, not that they're not fun now, of course, but the experience really is not quite the same. Unlike with a lot of my childhood reads, dimmed to hazy memories, I have a strong recollection of my first time reading James and the Giant Peach. Much as I loved Roald Dahl (personal favorites being the BFG, The Witches, Boy, and Matilda), I dreaded reading this book, p</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9237860542068256660">Oh, childhood, Roald Dahl takes me right back. I will always love Roald Dahl's work, because of how much these books meant to me as a kid, not that they're not fun now, of course, but the experience really is not quite the same. Unlike with a lot of my childhood reads, dimmed to hazy memories, I have a strong recollection of my first time reading James and the Giant Peach. Much as I loved Roald Dahl (personal favorites being the BFG, The Witches, Boy, and Matilda), I dreaded reading this book, popular as it was. In my younger years, completely different from now, I was a picky reader, wandering shelves, unsure what to read. Thus when an author tickled my fancy, I embarked on all of their books that I could get my hands on. As such, eventually the time came when I had to cave and read James and the Giant Peach.<br /><br />"But Christina," you might ask, "why did you not want to read this children's classic, much beloved by many you knew and by one of your favorite authors?" Well, my dear friends, the answer is simple. All my life, I have had a phobia of just about every kind of bug. My childhood self read that synopsis and looked at that cover and thought whatever the childhood equivalent of OH HELL TO THE NO was, which, I suppose, would be something in the vein of YOU CAN'T MAKE ME. Though precisely who would be trying to force me I have no idea, as my parents let me choose my own reading material.<br /><br />To try to keep what has already morphed into a rather long story from becoming a tome of giant peach proportions, I caved and read it, and, as ever, Roald Dahl charmed me utterly, perhaps more than usual because he won me over in spite of my stubborn, childish desire NOT to like the book. Ever since then, I've remembered James and the Giant Peach as a favorite. Rereading a book that meant so much to you as a tiny tot is always a treacherous prospect, because, sometimes, you discover that the book that so impacted you has all of the wit and charm of Mr. Collins.<br /><br />Of course, with Roald Dahl, you're pretty safe. In my case, I found that I could not enjoy this one nearly so much as an adult, but that I could still bask in the glow of Roald Dahl's boundless imagination. Seriously, that man was a freaking international treasure. How in the world did he come up with that? How did that brainstorming session go? "Mmm, this peach is delicious. Rather large. I wish I could live in a peach with my insect and arachnid friends..." The whole story runs with the absurd, making an art of it. This book would be a perfect transition to chapter books for kids who best love Dr. Seuss' wordplay and silliness.<br /><br />As an adult, I just found myself unable to lose myself in the magic of the tale the way I did as a child. I kept trying to impose logic where there was never meant to be any. Admittedly, some of the absurdities, like James' parents having been eaten by an escaped rhinoceros from the zoo, are quite humorous. Others, such as how the giant peach came into existence or the fact that sea gulls carried that peach across the Atlantic Ocean without stopping, my grown up brain could not just accept.<br /><br />The fact that Dahl wrote in an earlier time is very apparent in the peach's origin. James is just sitting around outside moping, when this weird man approaches him and offers him a bunch of squirmy little bright green things. He tells James to eat them so that something magical can happen to him. In the modern world, if a stranger gives you something weird like that, you better get to running and hollering your fool head off. Thankfully, James is a klutz and drops all of the green things, thus saving us from finding out what would have happened to him.<br /><br />Making the main characters James and a bunch of bugs now capable of rational thought was a clever way of allowing the child to shine. James, while exceedingly young, gets to be the problem solver, because, little life experience as he possesses, he knows more about the world than the insects do, aside from some biology lessons.<br /><br />Something that I entirely did not recall about this book was how much poetry Dahl wove into the story. Every few chapters, someone sings a song or uses a poem to express themselves. The songs made sense to me, but exposition as poetry did not, though I'm sure as a kid it's the best. At the end, James addresses all of the mucky mucks in America, all freaking out because the peach just landed King Kong style on the top of the Empire State Building, and calms them by introducing his friends in a poem. Sadly, this may be more efficient and logical than how governments actually function. Children will delight in these, I have no doubt, but I'm very picky about poetry.<br /><br />In all, James and the Giant Peach certainly did not impress me as much now, and I suspect that, for me at least, it's not his best. Still, he has imagination and humor like no other, and I imagine I will revisit this one again someday.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="9237860542068256660">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1495440959">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14572211417651106019">I can't seem to stop reading books by Roald Dahl! :D<br /><br />This time it's about a boy whose parents die so he has to live with two aunts, who are terrible people. They constantly mistreat him and keep him from meeting other children. <br />Just one small note here: Why are the bad people in Roald Dahl's stories always either enormously fat or very long and thin?! It's a constant throughout his stories and I wonder if there is a special reason that can be found in his life's story?!<br /><img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1452252356i/17667162.jpg" /><br />Another note here: A boy lo</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14572211417651106019">I can't seem to stop reading books by Roald Dahl! :D<br /><br />This time it's about a boy whose parents die so he has to live with two aunts, who are terrible people. They constantly mistreat him and keep him from meeting other children. <br />Just one small note here: Why are the bad people in Roald Dahl's stories always either enormously fat or very long and thin?! It's a constant throughout his stories and I wonder if there is a special reason that can be found in his life's story?!<br /><img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1452252356i/17667162.jpg" /><br />Another note here: A boy losing his parents having to live with ghastly relatives and his name is James Henry Trotter ... am I reading too much into this?!<br /><img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1452252356i/17667163._SX540_.jpg" /><br /><br />Anyway, one day there is sort of a miracle, making a peach on a tree in his aunts' garden grow (and to an enormous size too). Inside, James meets a lot of insects and soon they go on a trip together. Yes, this book basically is a giant road trip story.<br /><img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1452252356i/17667164._SY540_.jpg" /><br /><br />Again, the author demonstrates his huge ability to come up with the most fantastic events, funny rhymes and songs and he invented quite some marvellous creatures in this book. <br />The one star I deducted, by the way, was because I didn't like <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[that the sharks were made out to be the bad guys <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> since this teaches children reading the book a lie that has been spread for a long time, causing a lot of damage! Moreover, I also don't agree with the author that <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[the Cloud Man were bad or malicious - after all, it was the Centipede who started the trouble and then they shattered a beautiful rainbow, which would have caused me, too, to go after them! <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1452252356i/17667165._SY540_.jpg" /><br /><br />At first I was also a bit irritated that at the slightest inconvenience everyone was proclaiming THE END and started panicking but at the end of the story I started thinking ... What if THAT was the whole purpose of the story? I mean, whenever the creatures on/in the peach were panicking <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[James found a solution by staying calm and thinking <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> and in the end when <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[the companions had arrived in New York City, everybody there was panicking for no actual reason but a nice little talk (again, calm and civil) solved the problem. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />So maybe the moral here is that staying calm and using your head while keeping as cool as you can is the better way?! If so, it is marvellous.<br /><br />Oh, before I forget: yes, this book has Quentin Blake's cute illustrations again. And yes, they are as pretty as ever (FYI: mine are black and white, which I believe was their original form).<br /><img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1452252356i/17667166._SY540_.jpg" /><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer10177136663106118936">Membaca buku ini membuat gue kelaparan setengah mati.<br />Karena gue membayangkan buah peach kalengan raksasa, dengan warna oranye yang mengilap, tekstur buah yang lembut, air gula yang lengket, serta kadar gula yang sangat tinggi dan sanggup membunuh seekor brontosaurus karena terserang diabetes melitus secara mendadak. <br />Atau brontosaurusnya hidup tapi cuma boleh makan kentang rebus seumur hidup. <br />Tapi gapapa, karena brontosaurus cuma makan sayuran. <br />Tapi selama beberapa tahun kedepan, si brontosaurusn</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10177136663106118936">Membaca buku ini membuat gue kelaparan setengah mati.<br />Karena gue membayangkan buah peach kalengan raksasa, dengan warna oranye yang mengilap, tekstur buah yang lembut, air gula yang lengket, serta kadar gula yang sangat tinggi dan sanggup membunuh seekor brontosaurus karena terserang diabetes melitus secara mendadak. <br />Atau brontosaurusnya hidup tapi cuma boleh makan kentang rebus seumur hidup. <br />Tapi gapapa, karena brontosaurus cuma makan sayuran. <br />Tapi selama beberapa tahun kedepan, si brontosaurusnya akan jadi kurus. <br />Kasihan.<br />Ini brontosaurus siapa yang melihara sih, hey??<br /><br />Buah peach akan mengingatkan gue pada peach danish di toko kue Harvest. Ya Tuhan, keyboard gue penuh iler!! <em>*elap-elap*</em><br /><br />Gue suka cerita ini, walopun buat gue ngga jelas asal-usulnya kenapa Roald Dahl milih buah persik.<br /><em>Karena kalo duren, bau.<br />Karena kalo semangka, basah.<br />Karena kalo belimbing... bentuknya bintang.<br />Karena kalo pisang, kalo kelamaan ntar kulitnya item.<br />Karena kalo terong.... terong??</em><br /><br />Yeah okay, buah peach emang pilihan tepat deh.<br />Dan catchy. Maksud gue, coba aja bayangin ada buah peach terbang diangkut ama burung camar. Catchy banget kaaan??<br /><br />Ceritanya sangat lucu. Dan selalu deh, Roald Dahl itu tokoh utamanya anak-anak yang miskin ataupun yatim piatu. Yah mungkin itu emang ciri khasnya dia.<br /><br />Di cerita ini, sepertinya peach terbangnya James melewati pabrik coklat deh. Jangan-jangan punya Willy Wonka? hahaha</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_33939632">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/33939632" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/307208-lucy" class="left imgcol" title="Lucy"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1233177432p2/307208.jpg" alt="Lucy" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/33939632?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 19, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/307208-lucy" class="user" title="Lucy">Lucy</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer33939632">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15695247606289748165">I shouldn't be allowed to read classic children's literature. My brain simply doesn't appreciate its intended purpose - creativity, imagination, fantasy. Instead, I wonder, "What's the point here?" Sometimes, there is a point, but I think with Ronald Dahl, the focus is placed on the magic and if there happens to be a story in there somewhere in it..so be it.<br /><br />I borrowed it from the library because it was on the most-commonly-banned-books-in-America shelf and I wondered how the author of Charlie an</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15695247606289748165">I shouldn't be allowed to read classic children's literature. My brain simply doesn't appreciate its intended purpose - creativity, imagination, fantasy. Instead, I wonder, "What's the point here?" Sometimes, there is a point, but I think with Ronald Dahl, the focus is placed on the magic and if there happens to be a story in there somewhere in it..so be it.<br /><br />I borrowed it from the library because it was on the most-commonly-banned-books-in-America shelf and I wondered how the author of Charlie and the Chocolate Factory could upset so many modest American readers?<br /><br />I still don't know. Unless there is some upsetting symbolism that went way over my head and would be sure to go over the head of its intended audience, I don't understand how this book could offend anyone but mean, crusty old aunts who don't love and take care of their orphaned nephews.<br /><br />That being said, I wish I loved this non-controversial story, but I didn't. I thought it was overly strange. Strange in a did-you-write-this-while-tripped-out-on-LCD? kind of way. Giant talking bugs. Living in a peach. Flying over the Atlantic Ocean with the help of string tied onto 501 seagulls via the giant silkworm and spider. Landing on the needle of the Empire State Building in New York. A ladybug marrying a police man (What the?). O.K then.<br /><br />James was surrounded by mean, nasty aunts and now he's happy and living in a giant peach in New York City's Central Park. Wildly imaginative but famous through the ages?<br /><br />To make sure it deserved its fame, I had my eight year old son read it (even though the word a** is in it twice. Why did Dahl do that?) and he liked it. He didn't love it. Not because it was strange but because like most things he reads, the characters are magical and the plot bizarre. For him, it seemed almost standard fare. When he finished, he handed it back to me and I asked, "Did you like it?" "Yeah." That's it. No buzz. No acting out parts of the story and certainly no regurgitating details from it like he does with some of his other books.<br /><br />I guess we're just a bunch of fuddy-duddies around here because I found nothing extraordinary about James and the Giant Peach except it's eccentricity.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="15695247606289748165">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer258617844">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3973644645985250918">Dahl's imagination and writing skill is masterful. He transports many of us to wonderful fun worlds of adventure and peculiarity with characters that every child would love to meet. A conjurer of great bedtime stories and memories of being young and learning to read those first books.<br />He must be thee most famous writer. This tale of a boy escaping two bad ladies into a world of make believe via a giant peach is full of fun and thrilling moments. His writing so well done, the vocabulary at times i</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3973644645985250918">Dahl's imagination and writing skill is masterful. He transports many of us to wonderful fun worlds of adventure and peculiarity with characters that every child would love to meet. A conjurer of great bedtime stories and memories of being young and learning to read those first books.<br />He must be thee most famous writer. This tale of a boy escaping two bad ladies into a world of make believe via a giant peach is full of fun and thrilling moments. His writing so well done, the vocabulary at times is more than a child could understand. I had fun reading this as I never read this story before unlike the rest of his stories. I have seen the cartoon of this. Reading it was so much better due to the precious time I shared with my son in experiencing its narrative. This was a big achievement for me to actually complete a novel(novella) with the boy instead of mostly a collection of short stories and picture books. I do hope one day he can really take up reading with an even more passion unaided, and compete with me in the amount of time spent reading. <br /><a rel="nofollow" target="_blank" href="http://s1184.photobucket.com/albums/z322/LouPendergrast/?action=view&amp;current=71488dad.jpg">

<span id="freeTextContainer6088760892694172982">Well this is an other imaginative yet super disturbing Roald Dahl book. And THAT, my friends, is why I love his books so much! They're absolutely <em>loony</em> and a little bit scary. It's like Lewis Carroll's Wonderland psychotic mess meets a calm English breakfast. Spot o' tea, chaps? I think <a title="Charlie and the Chocolate Factory (Charlie Bucket, #1) by Roald Dahl" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6310.Charlie_and_the_Chocolate_Factory__Charlie_Bucket___1_">Charlie and the Chocolate Factory</a> and<a title="Matilda by Roald Dahl" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/39988.Matilda">Matilda</a> still win as my favourite Roald Dahl books, especially Matilda which totally empowers bookworms. YAY. CatCF is just a skewed crazy ride as well. BUT I LOVE IT. I DO. <br /><br />S</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6088760892694172982">Well this is an other imaginative yet super disturbing Roald Dahl book. And THAT, my friends, is why I love his books so much! They're absolutely <em>loony</em> and a little bit scary. It's like Lewis Carroll's Wonderland psychotic mess meets a calm English breakfast. Spot o' tea, chaps? I think <a title="Charlie and the Chocolate Factory (Charlie Bucket, #1) by Roald Dahl" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6310.Charlie_and_the_Chocolate_Factory__Charlie_Bucket___1_">Charlie and the Chocolate Factory</a> and<a title="Matilda by Roald Dahl" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/39988.Matilda">Matilda</a> still win as my favourite Roald Dahl books, especially Matilda which totally empowers bookworms. YAY. CatCF is just a skewed crazy ride as well. BUT I LOVE IT. I DO. <br /><br />SO! This is one of Dahl's more disturbing books since it starts off with a nice little boy getting looked after by his ugly, awful aunts. They abuse him and he cries and it's very Oliver Twist. SO. A weird (creepy) magic man gives James some magic seeds/beans/roots <em>something</em>...and then James spills them (accidentally) on a Peach tree. It grows into a massive massive peach, like the size of a house. Then James gets in, and it <em>freaking rolls over his awful aunts and kills them dead.</em> &lt;-- THAT is Roald Dahl for you peoples. DEATH TO VILLAINS. (It's a little disturbing.)<br /><br />The rest of the story is, as you can imagine, peachy. The sail the peach, float the peach, fly the peach, eat the peach. James meets bugs who become his friends. And theeeeen, they arrive in America and live happily ever after.<br /><br />My only problem with it is just the fact that: <strong>I have no idea what the heart of the story is.</strong> Don't bully kids because someday they'll kill you with a peach? If you're sad and lonely it can be fixed by meeting other children, living in a peach pip, and being not-sad-and-lonely anymore?? So I did struggle a bit with the point. <br /><br />Also: does anyone remember the '90s movie?!! I DO.<br /><img width="500" height="229" class="escapedImg" alt="description" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1426551789ra/14044416.gif" /></span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1066537612">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1066537612" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/3409532-april-does-feral-sometimes" class="left imgcol" title="aPriL does feral sometimes"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1451161499p2/3409532.jpg" alt="aPriL does feral sometimes" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1066537612?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 19, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3409532-april-does-feral-sometimes" class="user" title="aPriL does feral sometimes">aPriL does feral sometimes</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/18652536-james-and-the-giant-peach" title="Kindle Edition " class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/3409532-april-does-feral-sometimes?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3409532-april-does-feral-sometimes?shelf=humor" class="actionLinkLite">humor</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3409532-april-does-feral-sometimes?shelf=children" class="actionLinkLite">children</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3409532-april-does-feral-sometimes?shelf=adventure" class="actionLinkLite">adventure</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1066537612">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11324660145460708159">James Henry Trotter is a sweetie, and I couldn't adore this kid and his garden-friendly insect friends more. The illustrations were so cute.<br /><br />After enduring abusive aunts for three years who despise James after they agree to raise him from the age of four after his parents die, James meets a mysterious man in the garden who gives him a bag of magical rice-sized crystals. He spills the crystals accidentally near his aunts' sad peach tree losing every one. It's a catastrophe for James, who was hopin</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11324660145460708159">James Henry Trotter is a sweetie, and I couldn't adore this kid and his garden-friendly insect friends more. The illustrations were so cute.<br /><br />After enduring abusive aunts for three years who despise James after they agree to raise him from the age of four after his parents die, James meets a mysterious man in the garden who gives him a bag of magical rice-sized crystals. He spills the crystals accidentally near his aunts' sad peach tree losing every one. It's a catastrophe for James, who was hoping for something wonderful he could use. James has hit bottom.<br /><br />The next day, there is a giant peach growing on the tree! True to their vicious natures, the aunts start a business of charging tourists to see the peach, but lock up James in his room so he can't speak to anyone, especially kids. That night they release him from his room and order him to clean up the trash in the garden. Instead, he is so hungry from not being fed all day that he climbs into the peach to eat a bit when he discovers there is a tunnel into it. Crawling down the tunnel he discovers the huge peach pit - and the most adorable group of magically transformed bugs that anyone would be pleased to meet. Best of all, they love James. But this is not the end of the story. Meeting the insects is the beginning of new adventures and a new life, as they escape the evil aunts by rolling the peach away from the aunts' hilltop house. It isn't as easy as the reader might expect. They face trials and terrors in their escape which could mean their deaths. Even magically transformed beings can die! Will the friends work together and save each other by their individual strengths?<br /><br />Unlike many, I never read this when a child, or had it read to me. I'm very happy that I read it now. It is one of the most charming stories for young kids I've ever read, yet it completely recognizes the challenges of life and offers lessons of hope, perseverance, courage, friendship, inventive use of resources and imagination, while recognizing grief and child abuse. Kids will either ask you questions (most won't I suspect; they will take in the story the way they take in Little Red Riding Hood or Hansel and Gretel, unless you get uncomfortable or upset) that you can answer age-appropriately, or they will simply enjoy feeling safe and loved while you read to them. <br /><br />I recommend 'James and the Giant Peach' to anyone and everyone. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="11324660145460708159">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_41135945">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/41135945" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/30987-justine" class="left imgcol" title="Justine"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1186494631p2/30987.jpg" alt="Justine" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/41135945?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 28, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/30987-justine" class="user" title="Justine">Justine</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer41135945">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2286245714416626993">In the Pantheon that is Roald Dahl, I find that James and the Giant Peach gets the shaft. I don't think I've ever heard anyone mention it as their favorite, but after this most recent read, I've decided that Dahl's Odyssey includes some of the best parts of his writing. It is eerily bizarre, wonderfully imaginative, and filled with imagery that I still remember from the first time I read it. <br /><br />Tips Of My Hat:<br />~James is a cool-ass kid. The poverty/orphan combo that Dahl uses to play up how much we l</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2286245714416626993">In the Pantheon that is Roald Dahl, I find that James and the Giant Peach gets the shaft. I don't think I've ever heard anyone mention it as their favorite, but after this most recent read, I've decided that Dahl's Odyssey includes some of the best parts of his writing. It is eerily bizarre, wonderfully imaginative, and filled with imagery that I still remember from the first time I read it. <br /><br />Tips Of My Hat:<br />~James is a cool-ass kid. The poverty/orphan combo that Dahl uses to play up how much we love the protagonist works like a charm here. Poor James... <br />~Excellent villains. Aunt Sponge and Aunt Spiker are a nastier equivalent of the evil stepmother and stepsisters. Yikes.<br />~Comeuppance. Yes.<br />~A giant freaking peach. Dude, the peach gets infected by little crawly green goo and becomes a house.. and a cruise ship... and a plane. If I were stuck on that peach I'd probably be covered in hives, but damn it, I want one. <br />~Showing kids that bugs can be cool. The cast of giant, animated garden bugs is fascinating and shows not only Dahl's capability of turning the everyday into something magical, but also teaches children to consider others' points of view. Bravo Dahl, you sneaky bastard you.<br />~Some awesomely ribald poetry. "Once upon a time/When pigs were swine/And monkeys chewed tobacco/And hens took snuff/To make themselves tough..."<br />~The neat little after stories of what happens to all of the bugs.<br />~Some of the most beautiful imagery I can remember in Dahl-- the cloud men painting a rainbow and making hailstorms, the peach floating behind hundreds of seagulls, the ticker tape parade down Manhattan, the peach-pit house in central park. Yay!<br /><br /><br />Wags of my finger:<br />~The centipede is annoying as hell. They should have let him drown.<br />~500 seagulls are not poetic, they're gross.<br />~Once he's on the peach, James kind of becomes a little know it all. Sure, sure, he saves them all from the sharks and whatever, but honestly? At some point he just makes shit up and the others take him for his word. He's like.. 7 years old... <br /><br />James and the Giant Peach is light on the imperialism and heavy on the wonder. You go boy.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="2286245714416626993">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer1103435437407887058">**3.5 stars**<br /><br />Really, really cute story of a child that finds some unusual friends and adventures. I laughed out loud several times, specially with the centipede. If you're going to read this to your children though, just note that at some point the peach is confused with a giant bomb which is something you may want to keep in mind given the news lately. It's solved quickly and all ends well but I figured I should warn you anyway.<br /><br />Jeremy Irons was simply WONDERFUL with the narration. It gave me th</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1103435437407887058">**3.5 stars**<br /><br />Really, really cute story of a child that finds some unusual friends and adventures. I laughed out loud several times, specially with the centipede. If you're going to read this to your children though, just note that at some point the peach is confused with a giant bomb which is something you may want to keep in mind given the news lately. It's solved quickly and all ends well but I figured I should warn you anyway.<br /><br />Jeremy Irons was simply WONDERFUL with the narration. It gave me thrill to imagine such a serious looking guy narrating a book like this. :) Definitely try it out in audio if you want to read it.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer13114273012109368857">I grew up with a swelling admiration for Roald Dahl. I read everything and anything he would write until I was quoting text word for word. James and the Giant Peach was the one I treasured the most. I lived through the so lovely and ever innocent James as I did with almost every other adorable character Dahl went on to create. </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer11419242912917589305">This book was a highlight of my childhood, I remember it fondly and I look forward to reading it to my kids. Great imagery, great rhythm, great story for anyone who has ever felt lonely and wanted to escape to somewhere magical and wonderful. A giant peach is as good as any place. Highly recommended.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_734904805">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/734904805" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2492588-katie" class="left imgcol" title="Katie"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1406279836p2/2492588.jpg" alt="Katie" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/734904805?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 06, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2492588-katie" class="user" title="Katie">Katie</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/2492588-katie?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2492588-katie?shelf=fiction" class="actionLinkLite">fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2492588-katie?shelf=young-adult" class="actionLinkLite">young-adult</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer734904805">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6057976924730855269">If you are ever having a particularly bad day, I recommend that you steer yourself, as soon as possible, towards the children or young adult section of your local library. <br /><br />I took this advice this past Saturday, spurred on by a bad mood, a recent conversation with a friend and Roald Dahl, and a recent comment thread here on Goodreads recommending books for young, intelligent readers. It was amazing how much fun it was to leave the library with a stack of ten-odd books, a mix of old favorites (hel</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6057976924730855269">If you are ever having a particularly bad day, I recommend that you steer yourself, as soon as possible, towards the children or young adult section of your local library. <br /><br />I took this advice this past Saturday, spurred on by a bad mood, a recent conversation with a friend and Roald Dahl, and a recent comment thread here on Goodreads recommending books for young, intelligent readers. It was amazing how much fun it was to leave the library with a stack of ten-odd books, a mix of old favorites (hello again, <a title="A Wrinkle in Time by Madeleine L'Engle" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18131.A_Wrinkle_in_Time">A Wrinkle in Time</a> and <a title="The Phantom Tollbooth by Norton Juster" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/378.The_Phantom_Tollbooth">The Phantom Tollbooth</a>) and classics I've never gotten around to reading. In lots of ways, children's books do not mess around: they're storytelling distilled down to the heart of things, and the really good ones wave off attempts by others to suggest that stories apply to particular demographics. If a children's book, or young adult book, is good, it's still as good when you're thirty as when you're thirteen (although, maybe for different reasons). <br /><br /><em>"My dear young fellow," the Old-Green Grasshopper said gently, "there are a whole lot of things in this world of ours that you haven't started wondering about yet."</em><br /><br />This is pretty much <em>James and the Giant Peach</em>'s thesis statement.<br /><br />If I remember correctly, it's Roald Dahl's first book for kids, and the whole thing just brims with wonder at the universe. It's very delicately written so that it feels dreamlike but not artificial, and it manages to be funny, and openhearted, and adventurous. It's a master-class in constructing good, believable characters in a minimal amount of space. If I ever have kids, this will be one of their bedtime stories. I also like that it never talks down to the kids reading it. People die, and there are big words (and one or two curses) but on the whole it's a book that's kind and optimistic. <br /><br /><br />PS: New York City, it is ridiculous that you have not put up a peach pit somewhere in Central Park to commemorate this book. Get on that. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="6057976924730855269">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer14468827815627837488">Today is a day for revisiting old favourites, clearly. I have an excuse, I swear! I'm doing 20th Century Children's Lit next semester (for lack of decent medieval courses I haven't already taken, murr). So this is all relevant, right?<br /><br />Anyway, it's amazing how this book makes things as scary as <em>giant insects</em> fun and reassuring. It didn't work in general, but I was rather fond of Miss Spider. I smiled at the tiny bit of education about insects and their uses that gets worked into the story...<br /><br />My fav</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14468827815627837488">Today is a day for revisiting old favourites, clearly. I have an excuse, I swear! I'm doing 20th Century Children's Lit next semester (for lack of decent medieval courses I haven't already taken, murr). So this is all relevant, right?<br /><br />Anyway, it's amazing how this book makes things as scary as <em>giant insects</em> fun and reassuring. It didn't work in general, but I was rather fond of Miss Spider. I smiled at the tiny bit of education about insects and their uses that gets worked into the story...<br /><br />My favourite part is the Cloud-Men, painting a rainbow.<br /><br />I wonder if the man at the beginning who gave James the bag of magic things knew what would happen, really. It all turned out just as he said, marvellous and wonderful, and James was never lonely again, after all.</span>
