        <a href="/review/show/469928553?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 04, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2431873-emily" class="user" title="Emily">Emily</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/31646.James_and_the_Giant_Peach" title="Paperback 9780590505901" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/2431873-emily?shelf=childrens-books" class="actionLinkLite">childrens-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer469928553">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9611327123051912307">This book always scared me when I was younger. I also remember being terrified by the stop-motion movie (which in retrospect seems ridiculous, but what can you do). <br /><br />James' aunts are horribly frightening, as are most of Dahl's adult villains. I was also not into insects as a child. The idea of James in a peach with enormous bugs was not very palatable, and the ending - the peach house, right? - seems grotesque. <br /><br />I do enjoy these aspects of Dahl (the adult villains, the surreal plots), but I've nev</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9611327123051912307">This book always scared me when I was younger. I also remember being terrified by the stop-motion movie (which in retrospect seems ridiculous, but what can you do). <br /><br />James' aunts are horribly frightening, as are most of Dahl's adult villains. I was also not into insects as a child. The idea of James in a peach with enormous bugs was not very palatable, and the ending - the peach house, right? - seems grotesque. <br /><br />I do enjoy these aspects of Dahl (the adult villains, the surreal plots), but I've never been able to get over my distaste for this book. I haven't read it since I was 11 or 12, and going back to it doesn't sound appealing at all. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="9611327123051912307">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1226889650?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 14, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/10221719-ksenia-anske" class="user" title="Ksenia Anske">Ksenia Anske</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/10221719-ksenia-anske?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1226889650">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer531903767679879220">Just got done reading this, and I was roaring with laughter. At one point the dialogue between Centipede and Earthworm made me lose my voice, and I simply fell to the floor, gasping. Forget big important adult books. <br /><br />READ THIS FOR CHARACTER DEVELOPMENT.<br /><br />I swear by children's books. They are so simple and genius at the same time, you will see how it works, all the bones. It's amazing. I will read all of Dahl's work. And then, of course, as a perk, most of his books are illustrated by delicious Que</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText531903767679879220">Just got done reading this, and I was roaring with laughter. At one point the dialogue between Centipede and Earthworm made me lose my voice, and I simply fell to the floor, gasping. Forget big important adult books. <br /><br />READ THIS FOR CHARACTER DEVELOPMENT.<br /><br />I swear by children's books. They are so simple and genius at the same time, you will see how it works, all the bones. It's amazing. I will read all of Dahl's work. And then, of course, as a perk, most of his books are illustrated by delicious Quentin Blake.<br /><br />Now, as an added perk, if you read this 50th Anniversary edition, there is an interview with Roald Dahl at the very end. It was conducted 2 years before his death, and in it he talks about his writing process. It's fascinating.</span>

Mario, the son of Mama and Papa Bellini, proprietors of the subway-station newsstand, had only heard the sound once. What was this new, strangely musical chirping? None other than the mellifluous leg-rubbing of the somewhat disoriented Chester Cricket from Connecticut. Attracted by the irresistible smell of liverwurst, Chester had foolishly jumped into the picnic basket of some unsuspecting New Yorkers on a junket to the country. Despite the insect\'s wurst intentions, he ends up in a pile of dirt in Times Square. Mario is elated to find Chester. He begs his parents to let him keep the shiny insect in the newsstand, assuring his bug-fearing mother that crickets are harmless, maybe even good luck. What ensues is an altogether captivating spin on the city mouse/country mouse story, as Chester adjusts to the bustle of the big city. Despite the cricket\'s comfortable matchbox bed (with Kleenex sheets); the fancy, seven-tiered pagoda cricket cage from Sai Fong\'s novelty shop; tasty mulberry leaves; the jolly company of Tucker Mouse and Harry Cat; and even his new-found fame as &amp;quot;the most famous musician in New York City,&amp;quot; Chester begins to miss his peaceful life in the Connecticut countryside. The Cricket in Times Square--a Newbery Honor Book in 1961--is charmingly illustrated by the well-loved Garth Williams, and the tiniest details of this elegantly spun, vividly told, surprisingly suspenseful tale will stick with children for years and years. Make sure this classic sits on the shelf of your favorite child, right next to The Wind in the Willows.     <div class=" clearFloats bigBox" id="aboutAuthor"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/4273.Roald_Dahl">About Roald Dahl</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
      <a href="/author/show/4273.Roald_Dahl"><img alt="4273" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/authors/1311554908p2/4273.jpg" class="right" /></a>
    <div class="readable">
      
<span id="freeTextContainer8440625103644952771">Roald Dahl was a British novelist, short story writer and screenwriter of Norwegian descent, who rose to prominence in the 1940's with works for both children and adults, and became one of the world's bestselling authors.<br /><br />Dahl's first published work, inspired by a meeting with C. S. Forester, was Shot Down Over Libya. Today the story is published as "A Piece of Cake". The story, about his wartime a</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText8440625103644952771">Roald Dahl was a British novelist, short story writer and screenwriter of Norwegian descent, who rose to prominence in the 1940's with works for both children and adults, and became one of the world's bestselling authors.<br /><br />Dahl's first published work, inspired by a meeting with C. S. Forester, was Shot Down Over Libya. Today the story is published as "A Piece of Cake". The story, about his wartime adventures, was bought by the Saturday Evening Post for $900, and propelled him into a career as a writer. Its title was inspired by a highly inaccurate and sensationalized article about the crash that blinded him, which claimed he had been shot down instead of simply having to land because of low fuel.<br /><br />His first children's book was The Gremlins, about mischievous little creatures that were part of RAF folklore. The book was commissioned by Walt Disney for a film that was never made, and published in 1943. Dahl went on to create some of the best-loved children's stories of the 20th century, such as Charlie and the Chocolate Factory, Matilda and James and the Giant Peach.<br /><br />He also had a successful parallel career as the writer of macabre adult short stories, usually with a dark sense of humour and a surprise ending. Many were originally written for American magazines such as Ladies Home Journal, Harper's, Playboy and The New Yorker, then subsequently collected by Dahl into anthologies, gaining world-wide acclaim. Dahl wrote more than 60 short stories and they have appeared in numerous collections, some only being published in book form after his death. His stories also brought him three Edgar Awards: in 1954, for the collection Someone Like You; in 1959, for the story The Landlady; and in 1980, for the episode of Tales of the Unexpected based on "Skin".</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="8440625103644952771">...more</a>

    </div>
